The Sufficiency of General Possibility for Dimensions
Clarifies that possibility suffices for dimensions and defines general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere possibility (imkān) is sufficient for discussing dimensions, and the three cases covered by general possibility.

Core Idea
Dimensions need only be possible, not assumed: general possibility covers actual, contingent, and potential.

In this passage, the author argues that when we say bodies 'possess dimensions,' we do not require that these dimensions be actually assumed or present in a particular way. Rather, it is enough that it is possible for them to have dimensions. This possibility is understood broadly to include different states of actuality and potentiality. Let's examine the text to see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of possibility
Read the first two sentences which state that assumption is not necessary; mere possibility suffices. Also note that for abstract substances, dimensions are impossible.
Abstract substances here refer to non-material entities.
2
Learn the three cases of general possibility
The author mentions that 'general possibility' includes dimensions that are: necessarily actual (celestial spheres), contingently actual (elemental bodies), and purely potential (solid sphere). List these three and see how they differ.
Think about why a solid sphere is said to have dimensions only potentially.
3
Reflect on the teaching
Consider why the author emphasizes possibility over assumption. What problem does this solve? Discuss the implications for physical and metaphysical bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرض أيضاً ليس بلازم ، بل مجرد إمكانه كاف فمن المجردات يستحيل فرض الأبعاد بمعنى أن اتصافها بها من المحالات (١) التي لا يمكن فرضها. والظاهر أنه يكفي ذكر الإمكان أو القابلية، ولا حاجة إلى اعتبار الفرض . وذكروا أن المراد بهذا الإمكان هو الإمكان العام ليشمل ما تكون الأبعاد فيه حاصلة بالفعل لازمة كما في الأفلاك أو غير لازمة كما في العنصريات، وما يكون بالقوة المحضة كما في الكرة المصمتة فكلامهم يمثل تارة إلى أن المراد بالأبعاد تلك الامتدادات الآخذة في الجهات على ما هو حقيقة الجسم التعليمي. أعني الكمية القائمة بالجسم السارية فيه المحصورة بين السطوح حتى أن بين السطوح الستة للجسم المربع جوهراً هو الجسم الطبيعي، وعرضاً سارياً فيه هو الجسم التعليمي له أبعاد ثلاثة هي أجزاؤه لا بمعنى الخطوط إذ لو كانت فيه بالفعل لكانت في كل جسم بالفعل(وهذا غير الامتداد الذي هو الصورة الجسمية الحاصلة في كل جسم بالفعل) (٢) بحيث لا يلحقه التبدل والتغير أصلاً، وتارة إلى أنها الخطوط التي لا توجد في الكرة الساكنة إلا بالقوة المحضة بخلاف المتحرك كالفلك . فإن المحور عندهم (٣) خط بالفعل، وتارة إلى أنها السطوح والخطوط التي هي النهايات حيث نفوها عن الجسم الغير المتناهي، ولا خفاء في أنها ليست هي التي تتقاطع على زوايا قائمة، وإلا ظهر أن المراد بها الخطوط المتوهمة المتقاطعة التي هي الطول والعرض والعمق، وهي ليست بالفعل لا في الطبيعي ولا في التعليمي والانفصال الذي هو أيضاً بالقوة ليس مقابلاً له ليلزم كون الجسم ليس بمتصل بالفعل ، ولا منفصل بالفعل، بل للاتصال الذي هو حاصل بالفعل وفرق ابن سينا (٤) بين البعد والمقدار بأن البعد هو الذي يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين. ومن شأنه أن يتوهم فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين، فقد يكون بعد خطي من غير خط وسطحي من غير سطح كما في الجسم الذي لا انفصال في داخله بالفعل فإنك إذا فرضت فيه نقطتين فبينهما بعد خطي ولا خط ، وإذا فرضت خطين متقابلين فبينهما بعد سطحي ولا سطح، وذلك البعد الخطي طول والسطحي عرض فيظهر الفرق بين الطول والخط ، وبين العرض والسطح حيث يوجد الأول بدون الثاني، وإن لم يوجد خط بلا طول وسطح بلا عرض .
Assumption also is not necessary; rather its mere possibility is sufficient. For abstract substances, assuming dimensions is impossible, meaning that being characterized by them is among the impossibilities that cannot be assumed. And it appears that mentioning possibility or receptivity is sufficient, and there is no need to consider assumption. And they mentioned that what is meant by this possibility is general possibility to include that whose dimensions are actually present and necessary as in the celestial spheres, or not necessary as in the elemental bodies, and that which is in pure potentiality as in the solid sphere. So their words vary: sometimes to the effect that what is meant by dimensions are those extensions extending in directions according to the reality of the mathematical body, meaning the quantity subsisting in the body, pervading it, confined between surfaces, so that between the six surfaces of the square body there is a substance which is the natural body, and an accident pervading it which is the mathematical body having three dimensions which are its parts, not in the sense of lines, for if they were actually in it they would be in every body actually (and this is different from the extension which is the corporeal form present in every body actually) such that no change or alteration affects it at all. And sometimes to the effect that they are the lines that exist in a stationary sphere only in pure potentiality, unlike the moving one like the celestial sphere, for the axis according to them is an actual line. And sometimes to the effect that they are the surfaces and lines that are the ends, as they denied them of the infinite body. And it is not hidden that these are not those that intersect at right angles; otherwise it would appear that what is meant are the imaginary intersecting lines which are length, width, and depth, and they are not actual either in the natural or the mathematical [body]. And separation which is also in potentiality is not opposed to it so as to necessitate that the body is neither actually connected nor actually separated, but rather for the connection which is actual. And Ibn Sīnā distinguished between distance (buʿd) and magnitude (miqdār) in that distance is that which is between two non-meeting ends, and it is such that one can imagine in it ends of the type of those two ends. So there may be a linear distance without a line, and a surface distance without a surface, as in a body that has no actual separation inside it. For if you assume two points in it, between them is a linear distance but no line; and if you assume two opposite lines, between them is a surface distance but no surface. That linear distance is length and the surface distance is width, so the difference between length and line and between width and surface appears, since the former exists without the latter, although no line exists without length and no surface without width.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the concept of dimensions (abʿād) in bodies, addressing different interpretations and the distinction made by Ibn Sīnā between distance (buʿd) and magnitude (miqdār). The author begins by clarifying that in discussing dimensions, we do not require actual assumption (farḍ); mere possibility (imkān) is sufficient. For abstract substances (mujarradāt), assuming dimensions is impossible because being characterized by them is impossible. Then he notes that what is meant by possibility here is general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm), which covers three cases: dimensions actually present necessarily (as in celestial spheres), present but not necessarily (as in elemental bodies), and present only potentially (as in a solid sphere). The author then explains that scholars differ in what they mean by dimensions. Sometimes they mean the extensions (imtidādāt) in directions that constitute the mathematical body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī), i.e., the quantity that inheres in the natural body and is bounded by surfaces. In this sense, a square body has a natural substance and an accident (the mathematical body) with three dimensions that are its parts—not lines, because if lines were actual, they would be in every body. This is distinct from the corporeal form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) which is actually present in every body. Sometimes they mean the lines that exist only potentially in a stationary sphere but actually in a moving sphere (e.g., the axis). Sometimes they mean the surfaces and lines that are the ends (nihāyāt), which are denied of the infinite body. He then clarifies that these lines and surfaces are not those that intersect at right angles (length, width, depth); those are imaginary and not actual in either natural or mathematical bodies. Lastly, Ibn Sīnā distinguishes between buʿd (distance) and miqdār (magnitude): buʿd is what lies between two non-meeting ends, and one can imagine ends of the same type. For example, in a body with no internal actual separation, if you assume two points, there is a linear distance (buʿd khaṭṭī) between them but no actual line; similarly, if you assume two opposite lines, there is a surface distance (buʿd saṭḥī) but no actual surface. This linear distance is length (ṭūl), and surface distance is width (ʿarḍ). Thus, length and width can exist without actual lines or surfaces, even though no line exists without length and no surface without width.

Key Terms
إمكان عام (imkan amm)
بعد (bu'd)
جسم (jism)
فرض (fard)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for a body to have dimensions, those dimensions must be actually present and assumed.
Correction: The author clarifies that mere possibility (imkān) is sufficient; we do not need actual assumption. Dimensions can be potential.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that assumption (farḍ) is not necessary when discussing dimensions?
A) Because dimensions are always actual and need no assumption.
B) Because mere possibility (imkān) is sufficient for the discussion.
C) Because dimensions cannot be assumed in any body.
D) Because assumption leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
General possibility suffices for attributing dimensions to bodies, covering both actual and potential states.

Three Interpretations of Dimensions in Bodies
Presents three interpretations of dimensions: as extensions, as lines, and as boundaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main ways scholars have interpreted the term 'dimensions' (abʿād) in physical and mathematical bodies.

Core Idea
Dimensions are understood either as extensions of the mathematical body, as potential or actual lines, or as boundary surfaces.

Scholars have differed on what exactly is meant by 'dimensions' when describing a body. This passage outlines three distinct interpretations: (1) dimensions as the extensions that constitute the mathematical body, (2) dimensions as lines that are either potential or actual, and (3) dimensions as the surfaces and lines that form the boundaries of a body. Let's see how these views are presented and critiqued.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first interpretation
The first interpretation sees dimensions as the extensions of the mathematical body (jism taʿlīmī), which is an accident in the natural body. Note the distinction between natural substance and mathematical quantity.
The mathematical body is the quantity that pervades the natural body.
2
Study the second interpretation
The second interpretation views dimensions as lines. In a stationary sphere, these lines exist only potentially; in a moving sphere (e.g., the celestial sphere), the axis is an actual line.
This shows that the actuality of lines depends on motion.
3
Examine the third interpretation and the final note
The third interpretation sees dimensions as the surfaces and lines that are the ends (nihāyāt) of a body, which are denied of an infinite body. The author then clarifies that these lines and surfaces are not the intersecting ones (length, width, depth) that are imaginary.
The author also notes that separation (in potentiality) is not opposed to actual connection.

Source Text

الفرض أيضاً ليس بلازم ، بل مجرد إمكانه كاف فمن المجردات يستحيل فرض الأبعاد بمعنى أن اتصافها بها من المحالات (١) التي لا يمكن فرضها. والظاهر أنه يكفي ذكر الإمكان أو القابلية، ولا حاجة إلى اعتبار الفرض . وذكروا أن المراد بهذا الإمكان هو الإمكان العام ليشمل ما تكون الأبعاد فيه حاصلة بالفعل لازمة كما في الأفلاك أو غير لازمة كما في العنصريات، وما يكون بالقوة المحضة كما في الكرة المصمتة فكلامهم يمثل تارة إلى أن المراد بالأبعاد تلك الامتدادات الآخذة في الجهات على ما هو حقيقة الجسم التعليمي. أعني الكمية القائمة بالجسم السارية فيه المحصورة بين السطوح حتى أن بين السطوح الستة للجسم المربع جوهراً هو الجسم الطبيعي، وعرضاً سارياً فيه هو الجسم التعليمي له أبعاد ثلاثة هي أجزاؤه لا بمعنى الخطوط إذ لو كانت فيه بالفعل لكانت في كل جسم بالفعل(وهذا غير الامتداد الذي هو الصورة الجسمية الحاصلة في كل جسم بالفعل) (٢) بحيث لا يلحقه التبدل والتغير أصلاً، وتارة إلى أنها الخطوط التي لا توجد في الكرة الساكنة إلا بالقوة المحضة بخلاف المتحرك كالفلك . فإن المحور عندهم (٣) خط بالفعل، وتارة إلى أنها السطوح والخطوط التي هي النهايات حيث نفوها عن الجسم الغير المتناهي، ولا خفاء في أنها ليست هي التي تتقاطع على زوايا قائمة، وإلا ظهر أن المراد بها الخطوط المتوهمة المتقاطعة التي هي الطول والعرض والعمق، وهي ليست بالفعل لا في الطبيعي ولا في التعليمي والانفصال الذي هو أيضاً بالقوة ليس مقابلاً له ليلزم كون الجسم ليس بمتصل بالفعل ، ولا منفصل بالفعل، بل للاتصال الذي هو حاصل بالفعل وفرق ابن سينا (٤) بين البعد والمقدار بأن البعد هو الذي يكون بين نهايتين غير متلاقيتين. ومن شأنه أن يتوهم فيه نهايات من نوع تينك النهايتين، فقد يكون بعد خطي من غير خط وسطحي من غير سطح كما في الجسم الذي لا انفصال في داخله بالفعل فإنك إذا فرضت فيه نقطتين فبينهما بعد خطي ولا خط ، وإذا فرضت خطين متقابلين فبينهما بعد سطحي ولا سطح، وذلك البعد الخطي طول والسطحي عرض فيظهر الفرق بين الطول والخط ، وبين العرض والسطح حيث يوجد الأول بدون الثاني، وإن لم يوجد خط بلا طول وسطح بلا عرض .
Assumption also is not necessary; rather its mere possibility is sufficient. For abstract substances, assuming dimensions is impossible, meaning that being characterized by them is among the impossibilities that cannot be assumed. And it appears that mentioning possibility or receptivity is sufficient, and there is no need to consider assumption. And they mentioned that what is meant by this possibility is general possibility to include that whose dimensions are actually present and necessary as in the celestial spheres, or not necessary as in the elemental bodies, and that which is in pure potentiality as in the solid sphere. So their words vary: sometimes to the effect that what is meant by dimensions are those extensions extending in directions according to the reality of the mathematical body, meaning the quantity subsisting in the body, pervading it, confined between surfaces, so that between the six surfaces of the square body there is a substance which is the natural body, and an accident pervading it which is the mathematical body having three dimensions which are its parts, not in the sense of lines, for if they were actually in it they would be in every body actually (and this is different from the extension which is the corporeal form present in every body actually) such that no change or alteration affects it at all. And sometimes to the effect that they are the lines that exist in a stationary sphere only in pure potentiality, unlike the moving one like the celestial sphere, for the axis according to them is an actual line. And sometimes to the effect that they are the surfaces and lines that are the ends, as they denied them of the infinite body. And it is not hidden that these are not those that intersect at right angles; otherwise it would appear that what is meant are the imaginary intersecting lines which are length, width, and depth, and they are not actual either in the natural or the mathematical [body]. And separation which is also in potentiality is not opposed to it so as to necessitate that the body is neither actually connected nor actually separated, but rather for the connection which is actual. And Ibn Sīnā distinguished between distance (buʿd) and magnitude (miqdār) in that distance is that which is between two non-meeting ends, and it is such that one can imagine in it ends of the type of those two ends. So there may be a linear distance without a line, and a surface distance without a surface, as in a body that has no actual separation inside it. For if you assume two points in it, between them is a linear distance but no line; and if you assume two opposite lines, between them is a surface distance but no surface. That linear distance is length and the surface distance is width, so the difference between length and line and between width and surface appears, since the former exists without the latter, although no line exists without length and no surface without width.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the concept of dimensions (abʿād) in bodies, addressing different interpretations and the distinction made by Ibn Sīnā between distance (buʿd) and magnitude (miqdār). The author begins by clarifying that in discussing dimensions, we do not require actual assumption (farḍ); mere possibility (imkān) is sufficient. For abstract substances (mujarradāt), assuming dimensions is impossible because being characterized by them is impossible. Then he notes that what is meant by possibility here is general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm), which covers three cases: dimensions actually present necessarily (as in celestial spheres), present but not necessarily (as in elemental bodies), and present only potentially (as in a solid sphere). The author then explains that scholars differ in what they mean by dimensions. Sometimes they mean the extensions (imtidādāt) in directions that constitute the mathematical body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī), i.e., the quantity that inheres in the natural body and is bounded by surfaces. In this sense, a square body has a natural substance and an accident (the mathematical body) with three dimensions that are its parts—not lines, because if lines were actual, they would be in every body. This is distinct from the corporeal form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya) which is actually present in every body. Sometimes they mean the lines that exist only potentially in a stationary sphere but actually in a moving sphere (e.g., the axis). Sometimes they mean the surfaces and lines that are the ends (nihāyāt), which are denied of the infinite body. He then clarifies that these lines and surfaces are not those that intersect at right angles (length, width, depth); those are imaginary and not actual in either natural or mathematical bodies. Lastly, Ibn Sīnā distinguishes between buʿd (distance) and miqdār (magnitude): buʿd is what lies between two non-meeting ends, and one can imagine ends of the same type. For example, in a body with no internal actual separation, if you assume two points, there is a linear distance (buʿd khaṭṭī) between them but no actual line; similarly, if you assume two opposite lines, there is a surface distance (buʿd saṭḥī) but no actual surface. This linear distance is length (ṭūl), and surface distance is width (ʿarḍ). Thus, length and width can exist without actual lines or surfaces, even though no line exists without length and no surface without width.

Key Terms
جسم تعليمي (jism ta'līmī)
امتداد (imtidad)
خط (khatt)
سطح (sath)
نهايات (nihayat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mathematical body's dimensions are literal lines intersecting inside the body.
Correction: The author clarifies that dimensions are not lines; if they were lines, they would be actual in every body, which is not the case. Also, the intersecting length, width, depth are imaginary and not actual.

Checkpoint
According to the second interpretation, why does a celestial sphere have an actual line (the axis) while a stationary sphere does not?
A) Because the celestial sphere is divine.
B) Because the celestial sphere moves, making the axis actual.
C) Because the stationary sphere has no dimensions.
D) Because the axis is always imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The term 'dimensions' is interpreted in multiple ways among scholars: as extensions, as lines, or as boundaries, each with different implications for actuality and potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson distinguishes between natural body (substance) and mathematical body (accidental quantity). When you look at a physical object, do you ever separate in your mind its substance from its dimensions? How does this distinction challenge you to think about the reality of creation beyond mere physical appearance?
?
The passage says the imaginary intersecting lines (length, width, depth) are not actual. In your daily life, how often do you assume the dimensions you perceive are fully real and fixed? Could your assumptions about what is 'actual' be mistaken? What would it mean for your faith if some seemingly actual things are only potential?
?
Consider the idea that lines exist in potentiality in a stationary sphere but actuality in a moving one. Where in your own spiritual journey are you 'stationary' with only potential, and where are you 'moving' with actual lines of action? What needs to change so that your faith becomes actual rather than potential?

Ibn Sina's Distinction Between Distance and Magnitude
Ibn Sina distinguishes buʿd (distance) from miqdār (magnitude) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's distinction between buʿd (distance) and miqdār (magnitude) and how a linear or surface distance can exist without an actual line or surface.

Core Idea
Distance (buʿd) can be imagined without actual lines or surfaces; magnitude (miqdār) involves actual quantity.

Ibn Sina makes a subtle but important distinction between 'distance' (buʿd) and 'magnitude' (miqdār). While magnitude refers to the actual quantity of a body, distance is the interval between two non-meeting ends, and it can exist even when no actual line or surface connects them. This passage explains this distinction with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's definition of buʿd
Buʿd is defined as that which is between two non-meeting ends. It has the property that one can imagine ends of the same type within it.
Note the phrase 'non-meeting ends' – the ends do not coincide.
2
Understand the example of linear distance without a line
In a body with no actual internal separation, if you assume two points, there is a linear distance between them but no actual line connecting them.
This shows that distance is prior to any actual line.
3
Understand the example of surface distance without a surface
Similarly, if you assume two opposite lines, there is a surface distance between them but no actual surface.
4
Learn the implications for length and width
The linear distance is length (ṭūl) and the surface distance is width (ʿarḍ). Thus, length and width can exist without lines and surfaces, even though no line is without length and no surface without width.
This shows the priority of distance over the geometrical elements.

According to Ibn Sina, what is the relationship between linear distance (buʿd khaṭṭī) and a line (khaṭṭ)?
A) Linear distance is always accompanied by an actual line.

Defending the Definition of Body: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer about the intellect and dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intellect's ability to assume three dimensions does not make it a body, and how the author resolves the objection by distinguishing conceptual from external existence.

Core Idea
Acceptance of dimensions must be actual, not just imagined.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential challenge to the definition of body. The objector points out that the intellect (wahm) can imagine three dimensions, yet it is not a body. The author's answer clarifies that the definition requires real external receptivity, not mere conceptual supposition. Let's explore how this subtle distinction saves the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is raised. Note that the intellect can assume three dimensions but is not a body.
2
Understand the answer
The author responds that the acceptance of dimensions must be according to external existence. Pay attention to the analogy of 'moist'.
3
Apply to the soul
Consider how the soul, an abstract substance, can accept dimensions conceptually but not externally.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The conclusion is that only actual bodies have dimensions in originating existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (والمراد قبول أعيانها) أورد الإمام أن الوهم يصح فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيه، وليس بجسم فأجاب بأن المراد ما يكون كذلك بحسب الوجود الخارجي كما في قولهم الرطب ما يقبل الإشكال بسهولة، ولا خفاء في أنه تحقيق للمقصود بحيث لا يرد الاعتراض بالنفس التي هي جوهر مجرد يقبل الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة، وإلا فظاهر أن الوهم خارج بقيد الجوهرية، والحاصل أن المراد صحة فرض الأبعاد بحيث يتحقق الاتصاف بها ، وذلك في الوجود المتأصل لا غير
He said: 'And what is meant is the acceptance of their essences.' The Imām objected that the intellect (wahm) can correctly assume three dimensions in it, while it is not a body. So he answered that what is meant is that which is so according to external existence, as in their statement 'moist is that which accepts shape easily.' And it is not hidden that this is a realization of the intended meaning such that the objection does not apply to the soul (nafs), which is an abstract substance that accepts the three intersecting dimensions. Otherwise it is apparent that the intellect is excluded by the condition of substantiality. And the conclusion is that what is meant is the correctness of assuming dimensions so that being characterized by them is realized, and that is only in originating existence (al-wujūd al-muta'asṣil).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection (from the Imām al-Rāzī, likely) states that the intellect (wahm) can correctly assume three dimensions in itself, yet it is not a body. According to the definition that a body is that which can accept three dimensions, this would wrongly imply that the intellect is a body. The answer clarifies that the acceptance of dimensions meant in the definition is with respect to external existence, not mere conceptual supposition. The analogy given is the definition of 'moist' as that which easily accepts shape; this applies to external objects, not to the concept of moisture. Similarly, the body's receptivity to dimensions is real (in external existence). The soul (nafs) is an abstract substance that can accept the three intersecting dimensions (in a conceptual sense), but this does not make it a body because the acceptance is not in external existence. The condition 'substantiality' excludes the abstract intellect, which is not a substance in the relevant sense. Thus, the correct understanding is that the assumption of dimensions must lead to real characterization by them, and this only occurs in originating existence (al-wujūd al-muta'asṣil), i.e., bodies that actually exist in the world.

Key Terms
وهم (wahm)
نفس (nafs)
جوهر مجرد (jawhar mujarrad)
وجود خارجي (wujud khariji)
وجود متأصل (wujud muta'asṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something can be imagined to have three dimensions, it is a body.
Correction: The author clarifies that the acceptance of dimensions must be in external existence, not mere conceptual supposition.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the intellect (wahm) can assume three dimensions but is not a body?
A) He denies that the intellect can assume dimensions.
B) He states that the acceptance of dimensions must be according to external existence, not mere supposition.
C) He says the intellect is actually a body.
D) He redefines the intellect as a substance.

Key Takeaway
The definition of body requires actual external receptivity to dimensions, not just conceptual imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been content with merely knowing about a truth of faith without letting it shape your actions? What one belief do you hold intellectually but fail to live out? What will you change today to make that belief a reality in your external existence?
?
The lesson shows that the soul can conceive of dimensions without being corporeal. In what area of your life have you mistaken mental acceptance for actual submission? How can you move from imagining obedience to truly embodying it?
?
If your acceptance of Allah's commands remains only in your mind, you are like the soul that conceptually accepts dimensions but remains abstract. What command of Allah do you 'accept' in thought but resist in practice? What is holding you back from making it part of your actual life?

The Objection: Prime Matter and Dimensions
Objection that prime matter needs the form to accept dimensions, but form is not an acceptor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that prime matter can only accept dimensions conditionally upon accepting the corporeal form, and why this creates a problem for the role of form as an acceptor.

Core Idea
Prime matter needs the form to accept dimensions, but form cannot be an acceptor.

This lesson presents an objection raised against the view that prime matter is the sole substance that accepts dimensions. The objection argues that prime matter can only accept dimensions conditionally upon receiving the corporeal form, yet the form, being a principle of action, cannot be part of the acceptor. Understanding this objection is key to grasping the nuanced relationship between matter and form in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice the conditional statement: prime matter accepts dimensions only through the form.
2
Identify the key claim
The objection states that prime matter can assume three dimensions, but only if it first accepts the corporeal form.
3
Understand why the form cannot be part of the acceptor
According to the philosophers, the form is the principle of action and attainment, not of possibility and acceptance. Therefore, it cannot be an acceptor.
This is the logical problem.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The objection concludes that the only accepting substance is prime matter alone, which raises a difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اعتراضاته أن الهيولي جوهر يصح فرض الأبعاد الثلاثة فيها غايته أن قبولها للأبعاد يكون مشروطاً بقبولها للصورة الجسمية، ولا يجوز أن تكون الصورة جزءاً من القابل لما تقرر عندهم من أنها مبدأ الفعل، والحصول دون الإمكان والقبول، بل الجوهر القابل هو الهيولي لا غير وجوابه أن ما اختص الهيولي بقبوله هو الصور لا الأعراض من الكميات والكيفيات وغيرها كيف وقد صرحوا بأنه لاحظ للهيولي من المقدار : وإنما ذلك إلى الصورة فإنها امتداد جوهري به قبول الامتدادات العرضية على أنه قد سبق أن المراد بهذا القبول ما يعم الفعل ولو لزوماً، ولعل هذا الاعتراض بالنسبة إلى الصورة أوجه .
And among his objections is that prime matter (hayula) is a substance that can correctly assume three dimensions, except that its acceptance of dimensions is conditional upon its acceptance of the corporeal form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya). And it is not permissible for the form to be part of the acceptor (qābil) according to what has been established with them that it is the principle of action (mabda' al-fi'l) and attainment (ḥuṣūl) without possibility (imkān) and acceptance (qabūl). Rather, the accepting substance is prime matter alone. And his answer is that what prime matter is specific to accepting is forms (ṣuwar), not accidents from quantities, qualities, and others. How could it be? They have stated that prime matter has no share of magnitude (miqdār); rather, that belongs to the form, for it is a substantial extension (imtidād jawharī) by which accidental extensions are accepted, given that it has preceded that what is meant by this acceptance includes action even if necessary. And perhaps this objection is more pertinent with respect to the form.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and its answer. The objection concerns prime matter (hayula), which is a substance capable of assuming three dimensions, but only conditionally upon receiving the corporeal form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya). The problem is that the form cannot be part of the acceptor, since according to the philosophers, form is the principle of action and attainment, not of possibility and acceptance. Thus, the true acceptor is prime matter alone. The answer is that prime matter accepts only forms, not accidents such as quantities and qualities. The scholars have explicitly stated that magnitude (miqdār) does not belong to prime matter; it belongs to the form, which is a substantial extension (imtidād jawharī) through which accidental extensions are accepted. Furthermore, it was previously established that 'acceptance' in this context includes actuality even if only in a necessary sense. The author concludes that this objection is perhaps more appropriate when directed at the form rather than prime matter, implying that the form is the direct acceptor of dimensions.

قابل (qabil)
فعل (fi'l)

Misconception: One might think that prime matter directly accepts dimensions without any condition.
Correction: The objection clarifies that acceptance of dimensions is conditional upon receiving the corporeal form, but then argues that the form cannot be an acceptor, creating a problem.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The objection forces a refinement: prime matter cannot directly accept dimensions; it must do so through the form, but the form's nature as active prevents it from being an acceptor.

?
The philosophers held that form is the principle of action and attainment, while matter is passive and receptive. In your own spiritual life, are you more like a form—actively seeking and attaining—or more like matter—passively waiting for things to happen? Which attitude is more befitting of a servant of God, and how can you cultivate the proper balance?
?
This objection forces a refinement: prime matter cannot directly accept dimensions; it must do so through form. The Answer: Prime Matter Accepts Forms, Not Dimensions
Answer clarifying that prime matter accepts forms, and dimensions belong to the form as substantial extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the answer to the objection, which clarifies that prime matter directly accepts only forms, while dimensions are accepted through the form as substantial extension.

Core Idea
Prime matter is for forms; dimensions are for the form as substantial extension.

The answer to the objection resolves the tension by specifying what prime matter accepts. It explains that prime matter's role is to accept forms, not accidents like dimensions. Dimensions are accepted through the form, which is a substantial extension. This removes the contradiction about the form being an acceptor, because the form does not accept dimensions; rather, it is the extension through which dimensions are received.

Guided Path
1

3
Understand the form's role as substantial extension
The form is described as a substantial extension (imtidad jawhari) through which accidental extensions (dimensions) are accepted.
This is the key concept.
4
Note the refinement about 'acceptance'
It is mentioned that acceptance here includes action (fi'l) in a necessary sense, broadening the concept.
5
Consider the final remark
The author suggests the objection might be more pertinent against the form, implying the form is the direct acceptor of dimensions.

Meanings of Hadd and Rasam Across Disciplines
Provides lexical, logical, usuli, and Sufi definitions of hadd and rasam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of 'hadd' and 'rasam' in lexicography, logic, usul al-fiqh, and Sufism.

Core Idea
Hadd and rasam are defined differently by logicians, jurists, and Sufis.

This passage introduces the term 'hadd' as a boundary and 'rasam' as a definition, but their technical meanings vary by discipline. Understanding these distinctions is key to reading classical texts in theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Read the lexical meaning of hadd
Start by understanding the literal meaning of hadd as an endpoint or boundary. Notice how it is used for physical and temporal limits.
This foundation helps grasp figurative uses.
2
Explore the logical classification of rasam
Identify the two types of rasam in logic: complete (proximate genus + property) and incomplete (property alone or with distant genus). See the examples.
Contrast with hadd, which is a complete definition.
3
Compare usuli and Sufi definitions
Note that in usul, rasam is a subset of hadd; in Sufism, rasam means habitual character as traces of God's actions.
Each field repurposes the term.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these distinctions matter for reading scholarly texts. Misunderstanding can lead to confusion.
A single term can have multiple technical meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) الحد منتهى الشيء ويطلق على السطح أو الخط او النقطة التي تفصل بين منطقتين متجاورتين أو على النقطة التي تفصل بين زمانين تقول : حدود الدولة وحدود الأزمنة وللحد بحسب هذا التعريف معنى مجازي وهو دلالته على النقطة النقطة التي ينتهي  تقول : حدود السلطة التنفيذية، وحدود العلم، وحدود الصبر. : عندها امكان الفعل ، والرسم عند المنطقيين مقابل للحد وهو قسمان رسم تام ورسم ناقص، فالتام ما يتركب من الجنس القريب والخاصة كتعريف الانسان بالحيوان الضاحك والناقص ما يكون بالخاصة وحدها او بها وبالجنس البعيد كتعريف الإنسان بالضاحك او بالجسم الضاحك والرسم عند الاصوليين أخص من الحد، لأنه قسم منه ، وعند الصوفية هو العادة والخلق وصفاته لأن الرسوم هي الآثار وكل ما سوى الله تعالى آثار ناشئة عن أفعاله .
(1) Hadd: the end of a thing, and it is applied to a surface, line, or point that separates two adjacent regions, or to the point that separates two times. You say: borders of the state, boundaries of times. And hadd according to this definition has a figurative meaning: its indication of the point at which the possibility of action ends. And rasam for the logicians is opposite to hadd, and it has two types: complete rasam and incomplete rasam. The complete is composed of the proximate genus and the property, like defining man as 'the rational animal'. The incomplete is by the property alone or with the distant genus, like defining man as 'the one who laughs' or 'the laughing body'. And rasam for the usulis is more specific than hadd, because it is a part of it. And for the Sufis, it is habit, character, and attributes, because al-rusum (the traces) are the effects, and everything other than God are effects arising from His actions.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is an encyclopedic note on the terminology of hadd and rasam across different disciplines. It begins with the lexical meaning of hadd as a boundary or limit (e.g., borders of a state, boundaries of times) and its figurative meaning as the point where possibility ends. Then it explains that in logic, hadd and rasam are opposites, and rasam is divided into complete (composed of proximate genus and property, like 'rational animal') and incomplete (property alone or with distant genus, like 'laughing' or 'laughing body'). It notes that in usul al-fiqh, rasam is a subset of hadd. Finally, it gives the Sufi definition of rasam as habitual traits and character, since everything other than God are traces (rusum) of His actions.

The Problem with Defining Jawhar as a Genus
Al-Razi refutes jawhar as a genus using ontological arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argues that jawhar (substance) cannot be a valid genus for body, focusing on the issues of existence as a secondary intelligible and negation as an intrinsic attribute.

Core Idea
Existence is not intrinsic to quiddity; a negation cannot define a real essence.

This lesson begins a detailed debate on the definition of substance. Imam al-Razi challenges the idea that 'existent not in a subject' can serve as a genus for body. He argues that existence is added to quiddity and that the negation 'not in a subject' cannot be an intrinsic property. A key objection—that all universals are secondary intelligibles—is addressed and refuted.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ibn Sina's doubt
The passage opens by noting that Ibn Sina was uncertain whether the definition of jawhar is a hadd (definition) or rasm (description). This sets up the debate.
2
Examine al-Razi's first reason
Al-Razi argues that the definition of jawhar uses 'existent not in a subject.' Existence is a secondary intelligible without external reality, so it cannot be part of a real quiddity. Also, 'lack of need for a subject' is a negative attribute, not intrinsic.
This is a crucial ontological argument.
3
Consider the objection and response
An objector claims that all genera and universals are secondary intelligibles. Al-Razi distinguishes logical universals (which are secondary) from natural universals like 'body' and 'animal.' The response defends the legitimacy of natural universals.
This distinction is fundamental to Islamic logic and metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقل عن ابن سينا ما يشعر بأنه متردد في أن هذا حد أو رسم. وأبطل الإمام كونه حداً بأن الجوهر لا يصلح جنساً للجسم ولا قابلية الأبعاد فصلاً. أما الأول فلوجوه منها: أن الجوهر هو مفسر بالموجود لا في موضوع. والوجود زائد على الماهية لا ذاتي لها بل هو من المعقولات الثابتة التي لا تحقق لها إلا في الذهن فلا يصلح جزءاً للماهية الحقيقية، وعدم الاحتياج إلى الموضوع عدمي لا يصلح ذاتياً للموجود. لا يقال جميع الأجناس بل جميع الكليات من المعقولات الثانية. لأنا نقول المنطقيات منها لا الطبيعيات كالجسم والحيوان ونحو ذلك. ومنها أنه لو كان جنساً للجواهر لكان تمايزها لا محالة بفصول على ما هو شأن الأنواع المندرجة تحت جنس فتلك الفصول إما أن تكون جواهر فينقل الكلام إلى ما به تمايزها ويلزم التسلسل وإما أن يكون أعراضاً فيلزم تقوم الجوهر بالعرض وهو باطل لاستلزامه افتقار الجوهر إلى الموضوع، وأيضاً يلزم كون العرض محمولاً على الجوهر ونفسه بحسب الوجود على ما هو شأن الفصل مع النوع. وأما الثاني: فلأن معنى القابلية وإمكان الفرض وصحته ونحو ذلك من العبارات أمر لا تحقق له في الخارج، والالقام بمحل قابل له ضرورة أنه من المعاني العرضية دون الجوهرية، فننقل الكلام إلى تلك القابلية، ويلزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة. ضرورة توقف تحقق كل قابلية على قابلية أخرى سابقة عليها، ومثله باطل بالاتفاق، سيما وهذه السلسلة محصورة بين حاصرين، هما هذه القابلية والمحل. وأجيب عن الأول بأن الموجود لا في موضوع رسم لا حد، إذ لا حد للأجناس العالية، وعدم جنسية العارض لا يستلزم عدم جنسية المعروض. وعن الثاني: بأن كون فصول الجواهر جواهر، لا يستلزم افتقارها إلى فصل آخر، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان الجوهر جنساً لها أيضاً، لا عرضاً عاماً، كالحيوان للناطق. وعن الثالث: بأن الفصل ليس هو القابلية، بل القابل، أعني الأمر الذي من شأنه القبول، كالناطق للإنسان بمعنى الجوهر الذي من شأنه النطق، أي إدراك الكليات. لا يقال هذا نفس الجسم لا جزء منه، فكيف يكون فصلاً؟ لأنا نقول هو نفسه بحسب الخارج، وجزؤه بحسب الذهن، كما في سائر الفصول، هذا كله بعد تسليم امتناع كون العدمي جنساً أو فصلاً للماهية الحقيقية، وأورد صاحب المواقف بعد نقل هذه الأجوبة كلاماً قليل الجدوى جداً.
It was reported from Ibn Sina that he seemed uncertain whether this is a definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm). The Imam (al-Rāzī) nullified it being a definition by [arguing] that jawhar (substance) is not valid as a genus (jins) for body, nor is the receptivity of dimensions (qābilīyat al-abʿād) valid as a differentia (faṣl). As for the first [claim], [there are] several reasons: among them, that jawhar is explained as 'the existent not in a subject'. Existence (wujūd) is additional to quiddity (māhiyya), not intrinsic to it, rather it is among the secondary intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya) that have no actuality except in the mind, so it cannot be a part of the real quiddity. And 'lack of need for a subject' is a negative [attribute], not fit to be intrinsic to an existent. It cannot be said that all genera, rather all universals, are secondary intelligibles. For we say: the logical ones among them [are secondary], not the natural ones like body, animal, and the like. Also among them: if it were a genus for substances, then their differentiation would inevitably be by differentia as is the case for species falling under a genus. Those differentia are either substances, then the discussion shifts to that by which they are differentiated, leading to infinite regress; or they are accidents, then it would entail the subsistence of substance by accident, which is false because it necessitates the dependence of substance on a subject, and also it would require that the accident be predicated of the substance and itself existentially in the manner of the differentia with the species. As for the second [claim]: because the meaning of 'receptivity', 'possibility of supposition', 'validity', and similar expressions is something with no external actuality, and the placing in a locus capable of it necessarily is among the accidental meanings (maʿānī ʿaraḍiyya) rather than substantial. So we shift the discussion to that receptivity, and infinite regress follows in existing ordered things, since the actualization of each receptivity depends on a prior receptivity, and such is invalid by consensus, especially since this chain is bounded by two boundaries: this receptivity and the locus. And it was answered regarding the first: that 'the existent not in a subject' is a description, not a definition, for there is no definition for the highest genera, and the lack of genus for the accident does not entail lack of genus for the subject. Regarding the second: that the differentia of substances being substances does not entail their need for another differentia; rather that would be necessary only if substance were also a genus for them, not a common accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm), like 'animal' for 'rational'. Regarding the third: that the differentia is not the receptivity, but rather the receptacle (al-qābil), i.e., the thing whose property is reception, like 'rational' for man, meaning the substance whose property is speech, i.e., apprehension of universals. It cannot be said: this is the body itself, not a part of it, so how can it be a differentia? For we say: It is the same externally, and its part mentally, as in other differentia. All this after conceding the impossibility of a negative [attribute] being a genus or differentia for a real quiddity. And al-Sayyid al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī (Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif) after citing these answers, made remarks of very little benefit.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated debate over the definition of substance (jawhar) as a genus for body, and the role of receptivity (qābilīya) as a differentia. The author begins by noting that Ibn Sina was uncertain whether the given definition is a strict definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm). Imam al-Razi then argues that it cannot be a definition because substance is not a valid genus for body, nor is receptivity a valid differentia.

The first part of the argument addresses why substance cannot be a genus for body. Several reasons are given: (1) Substance is defined as 'existent not in a subject'—existence is a secondary intelligible with no external reality, so it cannot be part of a quiddity, and 'lack of need for a subject' is a negative property, not a positive intrinsic attribute. A potential objection is raised: all genera and universals are secondary intelligibles. The response distinguishes between logical universals (which are indeed secondary) and natural universals like 'animal' or 'body'. (2) If substance were a genus, then its species would be differentiated by differentia; those differentia must be either substances (leading to infinite regress) or accidents (implying that substance subsists by an accident, which is impossible because it would make substance dependent on a subject and entail that an accident is predicated of a substance like a differentia).

The second part addresses receptivity as a differentia. Receptivity, possibility, and similar notions have no external existence; they are accidental meanings. If receptivity is taken as a differentia, we must ask about its receptacle, leading to an infinite regress of receptivities, which is absurd.

After presenting these arguments, the text gives three responses. The first response states that 'existent not in a subject' is merely a description, not a definition, because the highest genera cannot be defined; moreover, the fact that an accident lacks a genus does not mean its subject lacks a genus. The second response argues that if the differentia of substances are themselves substances, that does not force a regress unless substance is a genus for those differentia (rather than a common accident). The third response clarifies that the differentia is not receptivity itself but the receptacle—the thing that receives. For example, 'rational' in man is not the attribute of rationality but the substance possessing rationality; externally it is the substance itself, intellectually it is a part. This distinction solves the problem of infinite regress. The passage concludes with a note that al-Sayyid al-Sharif's own remarks on these answers were of little use.

حد (ḥadd)

جنس (jins)
فصل (faṣl)
قابلية (qābilīya)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
معقولات ثانية (maʿqūlāt thāniya)
ماهية (māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the definition of substance as 'the existent not in a subject' is a complete and valid definition.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that it is only a description because it relies on a negative attribute and because existence is not intrinsic.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Razi argue that jawhar (substance) cannot be a valid genus for body?
A) Because substance is a secondary intelligible and lacks external reality.
B) Because the definition 'existent not in a subject' involves a negation, which cannot be intrinsic.
C) Because substance is itself a species, not a genus.
D) Because the differentia of body would lead to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Jawhar cannot be a genus because its definition involves a contingent existence and a negative attribute, neither of which can be intrinsic to a real essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not your own but a gift from Allah, how should that change the way you view your achievements and status? What would it mean to live as someone whose very being is contingent and received?
?
Al-Razi warns against mistaking mental concepts for real essences. Are there labels or identities you cling to—like 'successful,' 'pious,' or 'leader'—that are mere mental constructs rather than your true substance before Allah? How can you strip away those constructs?
?
The passage argues that a negation (like 'not in a subject') cannot define an essence. What negative statements do you use to define yourself—'I am not like them,' 'I am not a failure'? How might these negations prevent you from seeing your positive reality as Allah created you?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Regress Arguments against Jawhar as Genus and Qabiliyya as Differentia
Al-Razi presents two regress arguments against the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's two arguments: that if jawhar were a genus, its differentia would lead to infinite regress; and that if qabiliyya were a differentia, it would also lead to an infinite regress of receptivities.

Core Idea
Accepting jawhar as a genus or qabiliyya as a differentia leads to absurd infinite chains.

After establishing that the definition of jawhar cannot serve as a genus due to its ontological status, al-Razi presents two additional arguments. The first shows that if jawhar were a genus, its differentia would either be substances (leading to regress) or accidents (impossible). The second argues that receptivity (qabiliyya) as a differentia is problematic because it is an accidental meaning with no external reality, and its analysis leads to an infinite regress of receptivities.

Guided Path
1
Follow the differentia regress argument
Al-Razi argues that if jawhar were a genus for substances, then each substance species would be differentiated by a differentia. That differentia must be either a substance or an accident. Both options are untenable.
This is a classic reductio argument.
2
Examine the substance option
If the differentia is a substance, then we need another differentia to differentiate it, leading to infinite regress.
3
Examine the accident option
If the differentia is an accident, then substance would depend on accident, which is impossible because it would entail substance needing a subject, and an accident being predicated of substance like a differentia.
4
Analyze the qabiliyya regress argument
Al-Razi then argues that receptivity (qabiliyya) and similar notions have no external reality; they are accidental meanings. If they were a differentia, we would need to consider the receptacle of that receptivity, leading to an infinite chain.
The chain is bounded by the initial receptacle and the receptivity itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقل عن ابن سينا ما يشعر بأنه متردد في أن هذا حد أو رسم. وأبطل الإمام كونه حداً بأن الجوهر لا يصلح جنساً للجسم ولا قابلية الأبعاد فصلاً. أما الأول فلوجوه منها: أن الجوهر هو مفسر بالموجود لا في موضوع. والوجود زائد على الماهية لا ذاتي لها بل هو من المعقولات الثابتة التي لا تحقق لها إلا في الذهن فلا يصلح جزءاً للماهية الحقيقية، وعدم الاحتياج إلى الموضوع عدمي لا يصلح ذاتياً للموجود. لا يقال جميع الأجناس بل جميع الكليات من المعقولات الثانية. لأنا نقول المنطقيات منها لا الطبيعيات كالجسم والحيوان ونحو ذلك. ومنها أنه لو كان جنساً للجواهر لكان تمايزها لا محالة بفصول على ما هو شأن الأنواع المندرجة تحت جنس فتلك الفصول إما أن تكون جواهر فينقل الكلام إلى ما به تمايزها ويلزم التسلسل وإما أن يكون أعراضاً فيلزم تقوم الجوهر بالعرض وهو باطل لاستلزامه افتقار الجوهر إلى الموضوع، وأيضاً يلزم كون العرض محمولاً على الجوهر ونفسه بحسب الوجود على ما هو شأن الفصل مع النوع. وأما الثاني: فلأن معنى القابلية وإمكان الفرض وصحته ونحو ذلك من العبارات أمر لا تحقق له في الخارج، والالقام بمحل قابل له ضرورة أنه من المعاني العرضية دون الجوهرية، فننقل الكلام إلى تلك القابلية، ويلزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة. ضرورة توقف تحقق كل قابلية على قابلية أخرى سابقة عليها، ومثله باطل بالاتفاق، سيما وهذه السلسلة محصورة بين حاصرين، هما هذه القابلية والمحل. وأجيب عن الأول بأن الموجود لا في موضوع رسم لا حد، إذ لا حد للأجناس العالية، وعدم جنسية العارض لا يستلزم عدم جنسية المعروض. وعن الثاني: بأن كون فصول الجواهر جواهر، لا يستلزم افتقارها إلى فصل آخر، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان الجوهر جنساً لها أيضاً، لا عرضاً عاماً، كالحيوان للناطق. وعن الثالث: بأن الفصل ليس هو القابلية، بل القابل، أعني الأمر الذي من شأنه القبول، كالناطق للإنسان بمعنى الجوهر الذي من شأنه النطق، أي إدراك الكليات. لا يقال هذا نفس الجسم لا جزء منه، فكيف يكون فصلاً؟ لأنا نقول هو نفسه بحسب الخارج، وجزؤه بحسب الذهن، كما في سائر الفصول، هذا كله بعد تسليم امتناع كون العدمي جنساً أو فصلاً للماهية الحقيقية، وأورد صاحب المواقف بعد نقل هذه الأجوبة كلاماً قليل الجدوى جداً.
It was reported from Ibn Sina that he seemed uncertain whether this is a definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm). The Imam (al-Rāzī) nullified it being a definition by [arguing] that jawhar (substance) is not valid as a genus (jins) for body, nor is the receptivity of dimensions (qābilīyat al-abʿād) valid as a differentia (faṣl). As for the first [claim], [there are] several reasons: among them, that jawhar is explained as 'the existent not in a subject'. Existence (wujūd) is additional to quiddity (māhiyya), not intrinsic to it, rather it is among the secondary intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya) that have no actuality except in the mind, so it cannot be a part of the real quiddity. And 'lack of need for a subject' is a negative [attribute], not fit to be intrinsic to an existent. It cannot be said that all genera, rather all universals, are secondary intelligibles. For we say: the logical ones among them [are secondary], not the natural ones like body, animal, and the like. Also among them: if it were a genus for substances, then their differentiation would inevitably be by differentia as is the case for species falling under a genus. Those differentia are either substances, then the discussion shifts to that by which they are differentiated, leading to infinite regress; or they are accidents, then it would entail the subsistence of substance by accident, which is false because it necessitates the dependence of substance on a subject, and also it would require that the accident be predicated of the substance and itself existentially in the manner of the differentia with the species. As for the second [claim]: because the meaning of 'receptivity', 'possibility of supposition', 'validity', and similar expressions is something with no external actuality, and the placing in a locus capable of it necessarily is among the accidental meanings (maʿānī ʿaraḍiyya) rather than substantial. So we shift the discussion to that receptivity, and infinite regress follows in existing ordered things, since the actualization of each receptivity depends on a prior receptivity, and such is invalid by consensus, especially since this chain is bounded by two boundaries: this receptivity and the locus. And it was answered regarding the first: that 'the existent not in a subject' is a description, not a definition, for there is no definition for the highest genera, and the lack of genus for the accident does not entail lack of genus for the subject. Regarding the second: that the differentia of substances being substances does not entail their need for another differentia; rather that would be necessary only if substance were also a genus for them, not a common accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm), like 'animal' for 'rational'. Regarding the third: that the differentia is not the receptivity, but rather the receptacle (al-qābil), i.e., the thing whose property is reception, like 'rational' for man, meaning the substance whose property is speech, i.e., apprehension of universals. It cannot be said: this is the body itself, not a part of it, so how can it be a differentia? For we say: It is the same externally, and its part mentally, as in other differentia. All this after conceding the impossibility of a negative [attribute] being a genus or differentia for a real quiddity. And al-Sayyid al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī (Ṣāḥib al-Mawāqif) after citing these answers, made remarks of very little benefit.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated debate over the definition of substance (jawhar) as a genus for body, and the role of receptivity (qābilīya) as a differentia. The author begins by noting that Ibn Sina was uncertain whether the given definition is a strict definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm). Imam al-Razi then argues that it cannot be a definition because substance is not a valid genus for body, nor is receptivity a valid differentia.

The first part of the argument addresses why substance cannot be a genus for body. Several reasons are given: (1) Substance is defined as 'existent not in a subject'—existence is a secondary intelligible with no external reality, so it cannot be part of a quiddity, and 'lack of need for a subject' is a negative property, not a positive intrinsic attribute. A potential objection is raised: all genera and universals are secondary intelligibles. The response distinguishes between logical universals (which are indeed secondary) and natural universals like 'animal' or 'body'. (2) If substance were a genus, then its species would be differentiated by differentia; those differentia must be either substances (leading to infinite regress) or accidents (implying that substance subsists by an accident, which is impossible because it would make substance dependent on a subject and entail that an accident is predicated of a substance like a differentia).

The second part addresses receptivity as a differentia. Receptivity, possibility, and similar notions have no external existence; they are accidental meanings. If receptivity is taken as a differentia, we must ask about its receptacle, leading to an infinite regress of receptivities, which is absurd.

After presenting these arguments, the text gives three responses. The first response states that 'existent not in a subject' is merely a description, not a definition, because the highest genera cannot be defined; moreover, the fact that an accident lacks a genus does not mean its subject lacks a genus. The second response argues that if the differentia of substances are themselves substances, that does not force a regress unless substance is a genus for those differentia (rather than a common accident). The third response clarifies that the differentia is not receptivity itself but the receptacle—the thing that receives. For example, 'rational' in man is not the attribute of rationality but the substance possessing rationality; externally it is the substance itself, intellectually it is a part. This distinction solves the problem of infinite regress. The passage concludes with a note that al-Sayyid al-Sharif's own remarks on these answers were of little use.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an accidental differentia could be valid for substance.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that it would make substance dependent on an accident, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi argue would happen if a differentia of substance were itself a substance?
A) It would be fine.
B) Infinite regress would follow.
C) It would be an accident.
D) It would be a genus.

Key Takeaway
Both the differentia regress argument and the qabiliyya regress argument show that the proposed definition leads to absurdity, thereby refuting the definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi warns against accepting definitions that lead to infinite regress or rely on unreal concepts. Have you ever held a belief about Allah that was based on vague mental constructs rather than what is actually real? How would you begin to root out such unfounded notions from your faith?
?
The regress argument shows that if we don't stop at a proper foundation, we end in absurdity. In your own life, what are the foundations you rely on for your moral and spiritual decisions? Are they built on solid, real principles, or are they built on endless chains of justification that you haven't examined?
?
Al-Razi's argument requires rigorous intellectual honesty. When you study matters of creed, do you submit to the truth even when it contradicts your prior assumptions? Ask yourself: are you willing to accept that a cherished belief might be flawed if the evidence demands it?

Responses to al-Razi's Refutation
Three responses defend the original definition against al-Razi's critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main responses to al-Razi's arguments, which defend the definition of jawhar as a genus and qabiliyya as a differentia.

Core Idea
The responses refine the definition: it is a description, the differentia can be substances, and the differentia is the receptacle, not receptivity.

After presenting al-Razi's powerful criticisms, the text provides three responses that defend the original definition. The first response clarifies that 'existent not in a subject' is a description, not a definition, for the highest genera cannot be defined. The second response argues that the differentia of substances can be substances without regress if substance is a common accident, not a genus. The third response makes a crucial distinction: the differentia is not the abstract receptivity, but the concrete receptacle—the thing that has the capacity. These responses aim to salvage the definition while acknowledging al-Razi's points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first response: it's a description
The first response says that 'existent not in a subject' is a rasm (description), not a hadd (definition), because the highest genera cannot be defined. Also, the lack of genus for an accident does not affect the subject.
This subtly shifts the debate from definition to description.
2
Examine the second response: differentia as substances
The second response argues that if the differentia of substances are themselves substances, that does not force an infinite regress unless substance is a genus for them. But if substance is a common accident (like 'animal' for 'rational'), no regress follows.
This introduces the concept of 'common accident' (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm).
3
Analyze the third response: differentia is the receptacle
The third response distinguishes receptivity (qabiliyya) from the receptacle (qabil). The differentia is the receptacle itself, not the abstract capacity. For example, 'rational' in man is the substance that has the capacity for speech, not the capacity alone. Externally it is the whole, mentally it is a part.
This solves the regress problem by locating the differentia in the concrete entity.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark
The passage ends by noting that after these responses, al-Sayyid al-Sharif (the author of al-Mawaqif) made remarks of little benefit. This suggests that the responses are considered adequate by many scholars.

The third response clarifies that the differentia is not receptivity itself but the receptacle—the thing that receives. For example, 'rational' in man is not the attribute of rationality but the substance possessing rationality; externally it is the substance itself, intellectually it is a part. This distinction solves the problem of infinite regress. The passage concludes with a note that al-Sayyid al-Sharif's own remarks on these answers were of little use.

Key Terms

حد (ḥadd)
رسم (rasm)
جنس (jins)
فصل (faṣl)
قابلية (qābilīya)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
معقولات ثانية (maʿqūlāt thāniya)
ماهية (māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi's arguments are unanswerable.
Correction: The three responses provide valid defenses that reinterpret the definition.

Checkpoint
According to the third response, what is the correct understanding of the differentia in the definition of body?
A) The abstract receptivity.
B) The concrete receptacle.
C) The potentiality.
D) The accident.

Key Takeaway
The three responses defend the definition by reinterpreting it as a description, denying the inevitability of regress, and distinguishing receptivity from the receptacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself, do you rely more on your attributes (like your job, roles, or failures) or on your core substance—who you are at the deepest level? Which one will remain when everything else changes? Ask yourself honestly: what is the receptacle of your identity?
?
The passage ends with a dismissive note about some remarks being 'of very little benefit.' Reflect on the theological debates you engage in: are they truly clarifying or just 'of little benefit' to your faith and character? Would you be willing to set aside a long-held argument if it produced no spiritual growth?
?
The differentia is the thing itself, not an accidental property. In your relationship with Allah, do you treat your acts of worship as accidental additions to your life, or as the very expression of who you are? How might your day change if you saw your prayer not as an action but as the receptacle of your submission?

Five Positions on the Divisibility of the Simple Body
Enumerates five views on division of simple body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five philosophical positions on whether the simple body is divisible and whether division is actual or potential.

Core Idea
The simple body's divisibility has five possible modes: actual or potential, finite or infinite.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary lists the various opinions of philosophers and theologians on how a simple body can be divided. Before diving into which view is correct, it is essential to map out the logical possibilities. The author enumerates five distinct positions, each attributed to a school or philosopher.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting that it presents five distinct cases for the divisibility of the simple body. Each case is defined by whether the division is actual or potential and whether it is finite or infinite.
The passage begins by stating the simple body is divisible, then lists the five possibilities.
2
Identify the scholars
For each of the five cases, note which scholar or school is associated with it: the mutakallimun, al-Nazzam, al-Shahrastani, the majority, and Democritus.
Democritus' view is unique: small solid bodies divisible in imagination only.
3
Understand the taxonomy
Reflect on the logical structure: the classification uses two axes — actual vs. potential and finite vs. infinite — producing four possibilities, plus a fifth mixed view by Democritus.
This taxonomy helps organize the different philosophical positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقوال العلماء في قابلية الجسم البسيط للانقسام (المبحث الثاني) الفلاسفة [ الجسم البسيط قابل للانقسام، فإما أن تكون الانقسامات بالفعل متناهية وهو مذهب المتكلمين، أو غير متناهية وإليه ميل (۱) النظام (۲) ، وإما أن تكون بالقوة فقط متناهية، ونسب إلى الشهرستاني (۳)، أو غير متناهية، وهو رأى جمهور وإما أن يكون بعضها بالفعل، وبعضها بالقوة، وهو ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس من أن أجزاء البسيط أجسام صغار صلبة قابلة للقسمة الوهمية دون الفلكية، ثم اختلفت الفلاسفة فذهب المشاؤون منهم إلى أن الجسم يفتقر في قبول القسمة إلى مادة يتألف الجسم منها ومن الصورة الحالة، وغيرهم إلى أنه يقبل الانقسام بنفسه، فهو في نفسه بسيط كما هو عند الحس، وأما ما نسب إلى البعض من تألف الجسم من محض الأعراض فضروري البطلان.
Statements of the scholars on the divisibility of the simple body. (Second Section) The philosophers [The simple body is divisible. Either the divisions are actual and finite, which is the doctrine of the mutakallimun; or actual and infinite, which is the inclination of al-Nazzam; or only potential and finite, attributed to al-Shahrastani; or potential and infinite, which is the view of the majority; or some are actual and some potential, which is the position of Democritus, that the parts of the simple body are small solid bodies divisible in imagination but not in actuality. Then the philosophers differed: the Peripatetics held that the body, in accepting division, requires a matter from which the body and the form inhering in it are composed; others held that it accepts division by itself, so it is in itself simple as it appears to the senses. As for what is attributed to some, that the body is composed of mere accidents, it is necessarily false.]
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of philosophical views on the divisibility of the simple body (al-jism al-basit). It begins by stating that the simple body is divisible. Then it enumerates five possible cases: (1) actual divisions finite (mutakallimun), (2) actual divisions infinite (al-Nazzam), (3) potential only finite (al-Shahrastani), (4) potential only infinite (majority of philosophers), (5) some actual some potential (Democritus). The author then notes a further difference among philosophers: the Peripatetics (mashsha'un) hold that division requires a material substratum (madda) and form (sura), while others believe the body is divisible by itself and is simple in itself. Finally, the author dismisses as necessarily false the view that the body is composed solely of accidents. This establishes the conceptual framework for the subsequent discussion of the reliable doctrines.

المتكلمين

ديمقراطيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers agree on the nature of the simple body's divisibility.
Correction: This passage shows that there are at least five distinct views, differing on whether division is actual or potential and whether it is finite or infinite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which position holds that the divisions of the simple body are actual and infinite?
A) The mutakallimun
B) Al-Nazzam
C) Al-Shahrastani
D) Democritus

Key Takeaway
The simple body's divisibility is not a settled issue; there are five distinct logical possibilities based on actual/potential and finite/infinite.

Philosophers' Disagreement on Body Composition
Discusses Peripatetic vs. others on composition and rejects accident-composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Peripatetic view that division requires a material substratum and how it contrasts with other views, as well as why composition from mere accidents is false.

Core Idea
Philosophers differ on whether a simple body divides by itself or needs a matter-form composition.

After listing the five possible divisions, the author turns to a deeper philosophical question: what is the underlying nature of the simple body that makes division possible? The Peripatetics (mashsha'un) argue for a matter-form substratum, while others hold that the body divides by itself. The passage ends with a firm rejection of the idea that the body is composed of accidents alone.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two positions
Read the passage and identify the two opposing views: the Peripatetics require a material substratum (madda) and form (sura) for division, while others believe the body is divisible by itself and is essentially simple.
The Peripatetics are also known as the followers of Aristotle.
2
Examine the rejection of accident-composition
Note that the author declares as 'necessarily false' the view that the body is composed of mere accidents (a'rad). Reflect on why this might be considered absurd in the context of Islamic philosophy.
Accidents cannot subsist by themselves; they need a substance.
3
Connect to the earlier classification
Consider how this debate about composition relates to the five positions on divisibility. Does accepting one view on composition lead to a specific view on divisibility?
The Peripatetic view aligns with the idea that division requires a substratum, which may influence whether division is actual or potential.

Three Reliable Doctrines on the Composition of the Body
Presents the three accepted doctrines on the composition of the body: atoms, hyle and form, and pure simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical doctrines on the composition of the body and the common ground they share.

Core Idea
Three doctrines, one substratum: atoms, hyle-form, and pure simplicity.

In this lesson, we examine the three accepted views on how the body is composed. The author presents them succinctly, then points out that despite their differences, all acknowledge a fundamental substratum (hyle) upon which forms and accidents appear. Understanding these doctrines is key to later discussions in theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words stand before interpretation.
Notice the concise style of enumeration.
2
Identify the three doctrines
Find each of the three positions: (1) indivisible finite parts, (2) hyle and form, (3) pure simplicity. Note how they are introduced.
These are the 'reliable' (mu'awwal 'alayhi) views.
3
Examine the common ground
The author says all agree on a 'hyle' that receives forms and accidents. Understand this as a point of convergence.
The real dispute is about the nature of that hyle.
4
Reflect on the closing remark
The last sentence notes that the atomist view is close to the hyle-form view. Think about why the author might see them as similar.
This hints at a possible synthesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعول عليه من المذاهب ثلاثة . الأول : أن (۱) الجسم مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ متناهية . الثاني : أنه مركب من الهيولي والصورة. والثالث : أنه بسيط محض ، وكأنه وقع الاتفاق على أن هناك «هيولي» يتوارد عليها الصور والأعراض. وإنما النزاع في أنه الجسم نفسه، أو المادة التي تحل فيها الصورة، أو الجواهر الفردة التي يقوم بها التأليف، وإذا تحققت فالقول بكون الجسم من الجواهر الفردة والتأليف قريب من القول بكونه من الهيولي والصورة]
The relied-upon among the doctrines are three. First: that the body is composed of indivisible finite parts. Second: that it is composed of hyle and form. Third: that it is pure simplicity. It is as if there is agreement that there is a 'hyle' upon which forms and accidents successively come. The dispute is only whether it is the body itself, or the matter in which the form inheres, or the indivisible atoms from which composition arises. If you realize this, then the statement that the body is from indivisible atoms and composition is close to the statement that it is from hyle and form.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the three doctrines that are relied upon (al-mu'awwal 'alayhi) among the many views on the composition of the body. The author lists them: (1) The body is composed of indivisible finite parts (ajza' la tatajazzu' mutanahiya), i.e., atoms. (2) It is composed of hyle (hayula) and form (sura). (3) It is pure simplicity (basit mahd). The author then notes an underlying agreement: all acknowledge a 'hyle' upon which forms and accidents sequentially appear. The real dispute is about whether that hyle is identical to the body itself, or is the matter in which form inheres, or is the individual atoms from which composition arises. The passage concludes that the atomist view (jawhar fard) is close to the hyle-form view, suggesting a convergence.

Correction: The author shows they share a common ground on the existence of a substratum (hyle) upon which forms and accidents appear.
